				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell_Int%27l_Inc.&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Honeywell Int&#039;l Inc.">Honeywell Int&#039;l Inc.</a>)</span></div>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Honeywell_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Honeywell (disambiguation)">Honeywell (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Honeywell International Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">publicly traded</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(company)" title="Conglomerate (company)">conglomerate</a> company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> composed of four Business Groups—Aerospace, Building Technologies, Performance Materials &amp; Technologies (PMT), and Safety &amp; Productivity Solutions (SPS)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>—which produce <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> systems, industrial products, and engineering services.

class="external text" href="http://honeywell.com">honeywell<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Honeywell is a <a href="/wiki/Fortune_100" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortune 100">Fortune 100</a> company, and was ranked 77th in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The company has a global workforce of approximately 110,000, of whom approximately 44,000 are employed in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Honeywell_2018_10-K_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honeywell_2018_10-K-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The current <a href="/wiki/Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman">chairman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a> (CEO) is Darius Adamczyk.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p><p>The company's current name, Honeywell International Inc., is the product of a merger in which Honeywell Inc. was acquired by the much larger AlliedSignal in 1999. The company headquarters were consolidated with AlliedSignal's headquarters in Morristown, New Jersey; however the combined company chose the name "Honeywell" because of its brand recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-AlliedSignal_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-AlliedSignal-7">[7]</a></sup> Honeywell was a component of the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> index from 1999 to 2008; and prior to 1999, its corporate predecessors were included dating back to 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none"><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<p>The Butz Thermo-Electric Regulator Company was founded in 1885 when the Swiss-born <a href="/wiki/Albert_Butz" title="Albert Butz">Albert Butz</a> invented the <i>damper-flapper</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostat</a> used to control coal furnaces, bringing automated heating system regulation into homes.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup> The following year he founded the Butz Thermo-Electric Regulator Company. In 1888, after a falling out with his investors, Butz left the company and transferred the patents to the legal firm Paul, Sanford, and Merwin, who renamed the company the Consolidated Temperature Controlling Company.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup> As the years passed, CTCC struggled with growing debts, and the company underwent several name changes in an attempt to keep the business afloat. After it was renamed the Electric Heat Regulator Company in 1893, <a href="/wiki/W.R._Sweatt" class="mw-redirect" title="W.R. Sweatt">W.R. Sweatt</a>, a stockholder in the company, was sold "an extensive list of patents" and named secretary-treasurer.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>22</span></sup> On February 23, 1898, he bought out the remaining shares of the company from the other stockholders.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1906_Honeywell_Heating_Specialty_Company_founded">1906 Honeywell Heating Specialty Company founded</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1906 Honeywell Heating Specialty Company founded" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1906, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Honeywell" class="mw-redirect" title="Mark Honeywell">Mark Honeywell</a> founded the Honeywell Heating Specialty Company in <a href="/wiki/Wabash,_Indiana" title="Wabash, Indiana">Wabash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, to manufacture and market his invention, the mercury seal generator.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1922.E2.80.931934_mergers_and_acquisitions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1922–1934_mergers_and_acquisitions">1922–1934 mergers and acquisitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1922–1934 mergers and acquisitions" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>As Honeywell's company grew (thanks in part to the acquisition of Jewell Manufacturing Company in 1922 to better automate his heating system) it began to clash with the now renamed Minneapolis Heat Regulator Company. This led to the merging of both companies into the publicly held Minneapolis-Honeywell Regulator Company in 1927. Honeywell was named the company's first president, alongside W.R. Sweatt as its first chairman.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>The combined assets were valued at over $3.5 million, with less than $1 million in liabilities just months before Black Monday.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>49</span></sup> In 1931, Minneapolis-Honeywell began a period of expansion and acquisition when they purchased Time-O-Stat Controls Company, giving the company access to a greater number of patents to be used in their controls systems.
</p><p>W.R. Sweatt and his son Harold provided 75 years of uninterrupted leadership for the company. W.R. Sweatt survived rough spots and turned an innovative idea – thermostatic heating control – into a thriving business.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1934-1941_international_growth">1934-1941 international growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1934-1941 international growth" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Harold, who took over in 1934, led Honeywell through a period of growth and global expansion that set the stage for Honeywell to become a global technology leader. The merger into the Minneapolis-Honeywell Regulator Company proved to be a saving grace for the corporation.
</p><p>1934 marked Minneapolis-Honeywell's first foray into the international market, when they acquired the Brown Instrument Company, and inherited their relationship with the Yamatake Company of Tokyo, a Japan-based distributor.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>51</span></sup> Later that same year, Minneapolis-Honeywell would also start distributorships across Canada, as well as one in the Netherlands, their first European office. This expansion into international markets continued in 1936, with their first distributorship in London, as well as their first foreign assembly facility being established in Canada. By 1937, ten years after the merger, Minneapolis-Honeywell had over 3,000 employees, with $16 million in annual revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="In_World_War_II">In World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In World War II" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>With <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">the outbreak of war</a>, Minneapolis-Honeywell was approached by the US military for engineering and manufacturing projects. In 1941, Minneapolis-Honeywell developed a superior tank periscope and camera stabilizers, as well as the C-1 autopilot. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg/220px-Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="5223" data-file-height="3482"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 147px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg/220px-Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="147" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell_C-1_Autopilot_Control_Panel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A World War II-era Honeywell C-1 autopilot control panel</div></div></div>
<p>The C-1 revolutionized precision bombing in the war effort, and was ultimately used on the two <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">B-29</a> bombers that dropped atomic bombs on Japan in 1945. The success of these projects led Minneapolis-Honeywell to open an Aero division in Chicago on October 5, 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>73</span></sup> This division was responsible for the development of the formation stick to control autopilots, more accurate gas gauges for planes, and the turbo supercharger.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>79</span></sup> In 1950, Minneapolis-Honeywell's Aero division was contracted for the controls on the first US nuclear submarine, <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS Nautilus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>88</span></sup> The following year, the company acquired Intervox Company for their sonar, ultrasonics, and telemetry technologies. Honeywell also helped develop and manufacture the <a href="/wiki/RUR-5_ASROC" title="RUR-5 ASROC">RUR-5 ASROC</a> for the US Navy.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1950-1970s">1950-1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1950-1970s" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1953, in cooperation with the USAF Wright-Air Development Center, Honeywell developed an automated control unit that could control an aircraft through various stages of a flight, from <a href="/wiki/Taxiing" title="Taxiing">taxiing</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Takeoff" title="Takeoff">takeoff</a>, to the point where the aircraft neared its destination and the pilot took over for <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">landing</a>. Called the <b>Automatic Master Sequence Selector</b>, the onboard control operated similarly to a <a href="/wiki/Player_piano" title="Player piano">player piano</a> to relay instructions to the aircraft's <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">autopilot</a> at certain way points during the flight, significantly reducing the pilot's workload.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Technologically, this effort had parallels to contemporary efforts in <a href="/wiki/Missile_guidance" title="Missile guidance">missile guidance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numerical_control" title="Numerical control">numerical control</a>. Honeywell also developed the <a href="/wiki/Wagtail_(missile)" title="Wagtail (missile)">Wagtail missile</a> with the USAF.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg/220px-Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="2000"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 176px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg/220px-Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="176" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Honeywell-Pentax-Spotmatic</div></div></div><p>
From the 1950s until the mid-1970s, Honeywell was the United States' importer of Japanese company Asahi Optical's <a href="/wiki/Pentax" title="Pentax">Pentax</a> cameras and photographic equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>153</span></sup> These products were labeled "Heiland Pentax" and "Honeywell Pentax" in the U.S. In 1953, Honeywell introduced their most famous product, the T-86 Round thermostat.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>110</span></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell_thermostat.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Honeywell_thermostat.jpg/300px-Honeywell_thermostat.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="293" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1158"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 300px;height: 293px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Honeywell_thermostat.jpg/300px-Honeywell_thermostat.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="300" data-height="293" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell_thermostat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Honeywell thermostat</div></div></div>
<p>In 1961, James H. Binger became Honeywell's president and in 1965 its chairman. On becoming chairman of Honeywell, Binger revamped the company sales approach, placing emphasis on profits rather than on volume. He also stepped up the company's international expansion – it had six plants producing 12% of the company's revenue. He also officially changed the company's corporate name from "Minneapolis-Honeywell Regulator Co." to "Honeywell", to better represent their colloquial name. Throughout the 1960s, Honeywell continued to acquire other businesses, including Security Burglar Alarm Company in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>130</span></sup></p><p>The beginning of the 1970s saw Honeywell focus on process controls, with the company merging their computer operations with GE's information systems in 1970, and later acquiring GE's process control business.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>122</span></sup> With the acquisition, Honeywell took over responsibility for GE's ongoing <a href="/wiki/Multics" title="Multics">Multics</a> operating system project. The design and features of Multics greatly influenced the <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> operating system. Multics also influenced many of the features of Honeywell/GE's GECOS and GCOS8 <a href="/wiki/General_Comprehensive_Operating_System" title="General Comprehensive Operating System">General Comprehensive Operating System</a> operating systems. Honeywell, <a href="/wiki/Groupe_Bull" title="Groupe Bull">Groupe Bull</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a> formed a joint venture in <i>Magnetic Peripherals Inc</i>. which became a major player in the hard disk drive market. It was the worldwide leader in 14-inch disk drive technology in the OEM marketplace in the 1970s and early 1980s especially with its SMD (Storage Module Drive) and CMD (Cartridge Module Drive). In the second half of the 1970s, Honeywell started to look to international markets again, acquiring the French Compagnie Internationale pour l’Informatique in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>124</span></sup> Eight years later, Honeywell formed Honeywell High Tech Trading to lease their foreign marketing and distribution to other companies abroad, in order to establish a better position in those markets.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>147</span></sup> Under Binger's stewardship from 1961 to 1978 he expanded the company into such fields as defense, aerospace, and computing.
</p><p>During and after the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_Era" title="Vietnam Era">Vietnam Era</a>, Honeywell's defense division produced a number of products, including <a href="/wiki/Cluster_bombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluster bombs">cluster bombs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missile_guidance" title="Missile guidance">missile guidance</a> systems, <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_mines" class="mw-redirect" title="Land mines">land mines</a>. Minnesota-Honeywell Corporation completed flight tests on an inertia guidance sub-system for the X-20 project at <a href="/wiki/Eglin_Air_Force_Base" title="Eglin Air Force Base">Eglin Air Force Base</a>, Florida, utilizing an <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_F-101_Voodoo" title="McDonnell F-101 Voodoo">NF-101B Voodoo</a> by August 1963. The X-20 project was canceled in December 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Honeywell_project" class="mw-redirect" title="Honeywell project">Honeywell project</a>, founded in 1968, organized protests against the company to persuade it to abandon weapons production<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>In 1980, Honeywell bought Incoterm Corporation to compete in both the <a href="/wiki/Airline_reservations_system" title="Airline reservations system">airline reservations system</a> networks and bank teller markets.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Honeywell_Information_Systems">Honeywell Information Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Honeywell Information Systems" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg/220px-Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="501"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 161px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg/220px-Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="161" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Honeywell-Bull_DPS_7_Mainframe_BWW_March_1990.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1990 <i>Honeywell-<a href="/wiki/Groupe_Bull" title="Groupe Bull">Bull</a> Entry Level Mainframe DPS 7</i> <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe</a></div></div></div>
<p>On April 12, 1955, Minneapolis-Honeywell started a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" class="mw-redirect" title="Raytheon">Raytheon</a> called Datamatic to enter the computer market and compete with <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>118</span></sup> Two years later in 1957, their first computer, the <a href="/wiki/DATAmatic_1000" title="DATAmatic 1000">DATAmatic 1000</a> was sold and installed. In 1960, just five years after embarking on this venture with Raytheon, Minneapolis-Honeywell bought out Raytheon's interest in Datamatic and turned it into the Electronic Data Processing division, later Honeywell Information Systems (HIS) of Minneapolis-Honeywell.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>118</span></sup> Honeywell also purchased minicomputer pioneer <a href="/wiki/Computer_Control_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer Control Corporation">Computer Control Corporation</a> (3C's) in 1966, renaming it as Honeywell's Computer Control Division. Through most of the 1960s, Honeywell was one of the "Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs" of computing. IBM was "Snow White", while the dwarfs were the seven significantly smaller computer companies: <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>, Honeywell, <a href="/wiki/NCR_Corporation" title="NCR Corporation">NCR</a>, <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/UNIVAC" title="UNIVAC">UNIVAC</a>. Later, when their number had been reduced to five,<sup id="cite_ref-HOMC_page_143_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOMC_page_143-17">[17]</a></sup> they were known as "The <a href="/wiki/BUNCH" title="BUNCH">BUNCH</a>", after their initials: Burroughs, UNIVAC, NCR, Control Data Corporation, and Honeywell.
</p><p>In 1970 Honeywell acquired GE's computer business forming Honeywell Information Systems. In 1975 it purchased <a href="/wiki/Xerox_Data_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Xerox Data Systems">Xerox Data Systems</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/SDS_Sigma_series" title="SDS Sigma series">Sigma computers</a> had a small but loyal customer base. In 1986 HIS merged with <a href="/wiki/Groupe_Bull" title="Groupe Bull">Groupe Bull</a>, a global joint venture with Compagnie des Machines Bull of France and NEC Corporation of Japan to become Honeywell Bull. By 1991 Honeywell was no longer involved in the computer business.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1985.E2.80.931999_integrations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1985–1999_integrations">1985–1999 integrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1985–1999 integrations" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerospace_and_Defense">Aerospace and Defense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Aerospace and Defense" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>1986 marked a new direction for Honeywell, beginning with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Sperry_Corporation" title="Sperry Corporation">Sperry Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1990, Honeywell spun off their Defense and Marine Systems business into <a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">Alliant Techsystems</a>, as well as their Test Instruments division and Signal Analysis Center to streamline the company's focus.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Honeywell continues to supply aerospace products including electronic guidance systems, cockpit instrumentation, lighting, and primary propulsion and secondary power turbine engines. In 1996, Honeywell acquired <a href="/wiki/Duracraft" title="Duracraft">Duracraft</a> and began marketing its products in the home comfort sector.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p><p>Honeywell is in the consortium that runs the <a href="/wiki/Pantex" class="mw-redirect" title="Pantex">Pantex Plant</a> that assembles all of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear bomb">nuclear bombs</a> in the United States arsenal.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Honeywell Federal Manufacturing &amp; Technologies, successor to the defense products of AlliedSignal, operates the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Plant" title="Kansas City Plant">Kansas City Plant</a> which produces and assembles 85 percent of the non-nuclear components of the bombs.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Home_.26_building_controls"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_&amp;_building_controls">Home &amp; building controls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Home &amp; building controls" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Honeywell also began the SmartHouse project to combine heating, cooling, security, lighting, and appliances into one easily controlled system. They continued the trend in 1987 by releasing new security systems, and fire and radon detectors. Five years later, in another streamlining effort, Honeywell combined their Residential Controls, Commercial Systems, and Protections Services divisions into Home and Building Control, which then acquired the Enviracare air cleaner business.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>183</span></sup> By 1995, Honeywell had condensed into three divisions: Space and Aviation Control, Home and Building Control, and Industrial Control.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_control">Industrial control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Industrial control" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Honeywell dissolved its partnership with Yamatake Company and consolidated its Process Control Products Division, Process Management System Division, and Micro Switch Division into one Industrial Control Group. It has further acquired Measurex System and Leeds &amp; Northrup Company to strengthen its portfolio.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1999.E2.80.932002_merger.2C_takeovers"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1999–2002_merger,_takeovers">1999–2002 merger, takeovers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1999–2002 merger, takeovers" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="AlliedSignal_and_Pittway">AlliedSignal and Pittway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: AlliedSignal and Pittway" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/AlliedSignal" title="AlliedSignal">AlliedSignal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pittway" title="Pittway">Pittway</a></div>
<p>On June 7, 1999, Honeywell was acquired by <a href="/wiki/AlliedSignal" title="AlliedSignal">AlliedSignal</a>, who elected to retain the Honeywell name for its brand recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">[10]</a></sup> The former Honeywell moved their headquarters of 114 years to AlliedSignal's in Morristown, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>. While "technically, the deal looks more like an acquisition than a merger...from a strategic standpoint, it is a merger of equals."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-AlliedSignal_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-AlliedSignal-7">[7]</a></sup> AlliedSignal's 1998 revenue was reported at $15.1 billion to Honeywell's $8.4 billion, but together the companies share huge business interests in aerospace, chemical products, automotive parts, and building controls.
</p><p>The corporate headquarters were consolidated to AlliedSignal's headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey" title="Morristown, New Jersey">Morristown, New Jersey</a>, rather than Honeywell's former headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>. When Honeywell closed its corporate headquarters in Minneapolis, over one thousand employees lost their jobs. A few moved to Morristown or other company locations, but the majority were forced to find new jobs or retire. Soon after the merger, the company's stock fell significantly, and did not return to its pre-merger level until 2007.
</p><p>In 2000, the new Honeywell acquired Pittway for $2.2 billion to gain a greater share of the fire-protection and security systems market, and merged it into their Home and Building Control division,<sup id="cite_ref-:6_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-24">[24]</a></sup> taking on Pittway's $167 million in debt. Analyst David Jarrett commented that "while Honeywell offered a hefty premium, it's still getting Pittway for a bargain" at $45.50 per share, despite closing at $29 the week before.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Pittway's Ademco products complemented Honeywell's existing unified controls systems.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Electric_Company">General Electric Company</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: General Electric Company" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In October 2000, Honeywell (then valued at over $21 billion) accepted a takeover bid from then-CEO <a href="/wiki/Jack_Welch" title="Jack Welch">Jack Welch</a> of <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The American Department of Justice cleared the merger, while "GE teams swooped down on Honeywell" and "GE executives took over budget planning and employee reviews." However, on July 3, 2001, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>'s competition commissioner, <a href="/wiki/Mario_Monti" title="Mario Monti">Mario Monti</a> blocked the move.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This decision was taken on the grounds that with GE's dominance of the large jet engine market (led by the <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_CF34" title="General Electric CF34">General Electric CF34</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engine), its leasing services (<a href="/wiki/GE_Commercial_Aviation_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="GE Commercial Aviation Services">GECAS</a>), and Honeywell's portfolio of regional <a href="/wiki/Jet_engines" class="mw-redirect" title="Jet engines">jet engines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a>, the new company would be able to "bundle" products and stifle competition through the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_integration" title="Horizontal integration">horizontal monopoly</a>. US regulators disagreed, finding that the <a href="/wiki/Merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Merger">merger</a> would improve competition and reduce prices; United States Assistant Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_(attorney)" title="Charles James (attorney)">Charles James</a> called the EU's decision "antithetical to the goals of antitrust law enforcement."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honeywellinfo_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeywellinfo-29">[29]</a></sup> This led to a drop in morale and general tumult throughout Honeywell, and in turn, the then-CEO Michael Bonsignore was fired as Honeywell looked to turn their business around.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2002.E2.80.932014_acquisitions_and_further_expansion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2002–2014_acquisitions_and_further_expansion">2002–2014 acquisitions and further expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2002–2014 acquisitions and further expansion" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that portions of <i><a href="/wiki/David_M._Cote" title="David M. Cote">David M. Cote</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/David_M._Cote#Government_and_political_donations" title="David M. Cote">David M. Cote#Government and political donations</a>) be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> from it and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Merging" title="Wikipedia:Merging">merged</a> into this article. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:David_M._Cote#Splitting_and_Merging_proposal" title="Talk:David M. Cote">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(September 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018,_Friedrichshafen_(1X7A4422).jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018%2C_Friedrichshafen_%281X7A4422%29.jpg/220px-AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018%2C_Friedrichshafen_%281X7A4422%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="6720" data-file-height="4480"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 147px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018%2C_Friedrichshafen_%281X7A4422%29.jpg/220px-AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018%2C_Friedrichshafen_%281X7A4422%29.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="147" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AERO_Friedrichshafen_2018,_Friedrichshafen_(1X7A4422).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Honeywell <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a>, sold under the brand BendixKing</div></div></div>
<p>In January 2002 <a href="/wiki/Knorr-Bremse" title="Knorr-Bremse">Knorr-Bremse</a> – who had been operating in a joint venture with Honeywell International Inc. – assumed full ownership of its ventures in Europe, Brazil, and the USA. <a href="/wiki/Bendix_Corporation" title="Bendix Corporation">Bendix Commercial Vehicle Systems</a> became a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Knorr-Bremse" title="Knorr-Bremse">Knorr-Bremse AG</a>. Although declining in influence, Honeywell maintains a presence in emerging industries, such as Northern <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a>. Honeywell's Plant integrator is currently deployed in some of the most important plant-sites in the Oil Sands (<a href="/wiki/Syncrude" title="Syncrude">Syncrude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suncor" class="mw-redirect" title="Suncor">Suncor</a>, and others). In February that year, Honeywell's board appointed their next CEO and chairman, David M. Cote. Cote was instrumental in uniting the company cultures of Honeywell, AlliedSignal, and Pittway. Since 2002, Honeywell has made more than 80 acquisitions and 60 divestures, while adding $12 billion in new sales<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and increasing its labor force to 131,000 as a result of these acquisitions. Under his tenure, Honeywell's stock has nearly tripled from $35.23 in April 2002 to $99.39 as of January 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p><p>Honeywell made a £1.2bn ($2.3bn) bid for <a href="/wiki/Novar_plc" title="Novar plc">Novar plc</a> in December 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The acquisition was finalized on March 31, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Novar_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novar-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In October 2005, Honeywell bought out <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical_Company" title="Dow Chemical Company">Dow's</a> 50% stake in <a href="/wiki/UOP_LLC" title="UOP LLC">UOP</a> for $825 million, giving them complete control over the joint venture in petrochemical and refining technology.<sup id="cite_ref-:UOP2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:UOP2-36">[36]</a></sup> In May 2010, Honeywell outbid UK-based <a href="/wiki/Cinven" title="Cinven">Cinven</a> and acquired the French company Sperian Protection for $1.4 billion, which was then incorporated into its automation and controls safety unit.<sup id="cite_ref-:72_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:72-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2015-present">2015-present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 2015-present" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, the headquarters were moved to <a href="/wiki/Morris_Plains,_New_Jersey" title="Morris Plains, New Jersey">Morris Plains, New Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> On July 1, 2019, Honeywell moved employees into a temporary headquarters building in Charlotte before their new building was complete.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The 475,000-square-foot building on 40 acres in Morris Plains features state-of-the-art technology and greater energy efficiency than Honeywell's Morristown campus, which was underutilized, outdated and costly, according to Cote.
</p><p>On December 29, 2015, Honeywell completed the acquisition of Elster for US$5.1B (announced on July 28, 2015) entering the space of gas, electricity, and water meters with a specific focus on smart meters and hoped to be a growth driver for Honeywell in 2016 and beyond. The deal also complements the HON Combustion business with the addition of Elster with strong brands such as Kromschroeder and Eclipse. Honeywell International Inc. then acquired the 30% stake in UOP Russell LLC it didn't own already for roughly $240 million in January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In February, Honeywell entered into a definitive agreement to acquire Xtralis, a leading global provider of aspirating smoke detection along with advanced perimeter security technologies and video analytics software, for $480 million from funds advised by Pacific Equity Partners and Blum Capital Partners. The deal was completed on April 1, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In May 2016, Honeywell International Inc. settled its patent dispute regarding Google subsidiary Nest Labs, whose thermostats Honeywell claimed infringed on several of its patents. Google parent Alphabet Inc. and Honeywell said they reached a "patent cross-license" agreement that "fully resolves" the long-standing dispute. Honeywell sued Nest Labs in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 2017, Honeywell opened a new software center in Atlanta, Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-Trubey_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trubey-43">[43]</a></sup></p><p>David Cote stepped down as CEO on April 1, 2017, and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Darius_Adamczyk" title="Darius Adamczyk">Darius Adamczyk</a>, who had been promoted to president and <a href="/wiki/Chief_operating_officer" title="Chief operating officer">chief operating officer</a> (COO) the previous year. Cote served as executive chairman through April 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_-_stepping_down_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_-_stepping_down-44">[44]</a></sup> On October 10, 2017, Honeywell announced plans to spinoff its Homes, ADI Global Distribution, and Transportation Systems businesses into two separate, publicly traded companies by the end of 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p><p>In 2018, Honeywell spun off both Honeywell Turbo Technologies (now <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Advancing_Motion" title="Garrett Advancing Motion">Garrett Advancing Motion</a>) and its consumer products business (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Resideo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Resideo (page does not exist)">Resideo</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-:9_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:10_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-47">[47]</a></sup> both companies are publicly traded on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>. For the fiscal year 2018, Honeywell reported net income of US$6.765 billion, with an annual revenue of US$41.802 billion, an increase of 3.13% over the previous fiscal cycle. Honeywell's shares traded at over $169 per share, and its market capitalization was valued at over US$120.26 billion in September 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Honeywell_2018_10-K_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honeywell_2018_10-K-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p><p>In 2019, Honeywell:
</p>
<ul><li>Launched Honeywell Forge, an <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Internet_of_Things" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial Internet of Things">Industrial Internet of Things</a> analytics platform.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Relocated its corporate headquarters to Charlotte, North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Honeywell moved employees into a temporary headquarters building in Charlotte before their new building was complete.</li>
<li>Established Honeywell Robotics in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> to focus on warehouse automation, working in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li></ul><p>In March of 2020, Honeywell announced that it was on the verge of making the largest <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computer" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum computer">quantum computer</a> ever built.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_Groups">Business Groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Business Groups" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<p>The company operates four business groups – <b>Honeywell Aerospace</b>, <b>Honeywell Building Technologies</b>, <b>Safety and Productivity Solutions (SPS)</b>, and <b>Performance Materials and Technologies (PMT)</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Honeywell_2018_10-K_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honeywell_2018_10-K-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p>Business units within the company are as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_governance">Corporate governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Corporate governance" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Products_and_services">Products and services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Products and services" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg/220px-Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="893" data-file-height="723"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 178px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg/220px-Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="178" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Digital_Compass_sensor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Honeywell digital compass sensor mounted on a <a href="/wiki/Circuit_board" class="mw-redirect" title="Circuit board">circuit board</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Honeywell_products_and_services" title="List of Honeywell products and services">List of Honeywell products and services</a></div>
<p>Honeywell has many brands that commercial and retail consumers may recognize, including its line of home <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostats</a> (particularly the iconic round type) and <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Advancing_Motion" title="Garrett Advancing Motion">Garrett</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbochargers</a>. In addition to consumer home products, Honeywell produces thermostats, sensors, security alarm systems, and air cleaners and <a href="/wiki/Dehumidifiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Dehumidifiers">dehumidifiers</a>. The company also licenses its brand name for use in various retail products made by other manufacturers, including air conditioners, heaters, fans, security safes, home generators, and paper shredders.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft">Aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Aircraft" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Honeywell_RQ-16_T-Hawk" title="Honeywell RQ-16 T-Hawk">Honeywell RQ-16 T-Hawk</a></li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Missiles_and_rockets">Missiles and rockets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Missiles and rockets" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/RUR-5_ASROC" title="RUR-5 ASROC">RUR-5 ASROC</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wagtail_(missile)" title="Wagtail (missile)">Wagtail (missile)</a></li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Honeywell_Scanning_and_Mobility">Honeywell Scanning and Mobility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Honeywell Scanning and Mobility" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li>Honeywell AIDC products</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Intermec" title="Intermec">Intermec Products</a></li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_since_2002">Acquisitions since 2002</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Acquisitions since 2002" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Honeywell's acquisitions have consisted largely of businesses aligned with the company's existing technologies. The acquired companies are integrated into one of Honeywell's four business groups (Aerospace, Home and Building Technologies (HBT), Safety and Productivity Solutions (SPS), or Performance Materials and Technologies (PMT)) but retain their original brand name.
</p>

<td>HBT

<td>HBT
</td></tr></tbody></table></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Environmental record" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> states that no corporation has been linked to a greater number of <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> toxic waste sites than has Honeywell.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Honeywell ranks 44th in a list of US corporations most responsible for air pollution, releasing more than 4.25 million kg (9.4 million pounds) of toxins per year into the air.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> In 2001, Honeywell agreed to pay $150,000 in <a href="/wiki/Civil_penalty" title="Civil penalty">civil penalties</a> and to perform $772,000 worth of reparations for environmental violations involving:<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<ul><li>failure to prevent or repair leaks of hazardous organic pollutants into the air</li>
<li>failure to repair or report refrigeration equipment containing <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a></li>
<li>inadequate reporting of <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">dichlorodifluoromethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caprolactam" title="Caprolactam">caprolactam</a> emissions</li></ul><p>In 2003, a federal judge in <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark, New Jersey</a>, ordered the company to perform an estimated $400 million <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">environmental remediation</a> of chromium waste, citing "a substantial risk of imminent damage to public health and safety and imminent and severe damage to the environment."<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In the same year, Honeywell paid $3.6 million to avoid a federal trial regarding its responsibility for <a href="/wiki/Trichloroethylene" title="Trichloroethylene">trichloroethylene</a> contamination in <a href="/wiki/Lisle,_Illinois" title="Lisle, Illinois">Lisle, Illinois</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/State_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="State of New York">State of New York</a> announced that it would require Honeywell to complete an estimated $448 million cleanup of more than 74,000 kg (165,000 lbs) of mercury and other toxic waste dumped into <a href="/wiki/Onondaga_Lake" title="Onondaga Lake">Onondaga Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_NY" class="mw-redirect" title="Syracuse, NY">Syracuse, NY</a> from a former Allied Chemical property.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Honeywell established three water treatment plants by November 2014, and the chemicals cleanup site removed 7 tons of mercury.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In November 2015, Audubon New York gave the Thomas W. Keesee, Jr. Conservation Award to Honeywell for its cleanup efforts in “one of the most ambitious environmental reclamation projects in the United States.”<sup id="cite_ref-Audubon_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audubon-145">[145]</a></sup> By December 2017, Honeywell completed dredging the lake<sup id="cite_ref-Coin_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coin-146">[146]</a></sup> and, later that month, the Department of Justice filed a settlement requiring Honeywell to pay a separate $9.5 million in damages, as well build 20 restoration projects on the shore to help repair the greater area surrounding the lake.<sup id="cite_ref-Coin_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coin-146">[146]</a></sup></p><p>In 2005, the state of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> sued Honeywell, <a href="/wiki/Occidental_Petroleum" title="Occidental Petroleum">Occidental Petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/PPG_Industries" title="PPG Industries">PPG</a> to compel cleanup of more than 100 sites contaminated with chromium, a metal linked to lung cancer, <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" class="mw-redirect" title="Peptic ulcer">ulcers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In 2008, the state of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> made a settlement with Honeywell to pay a $5 million fine and contribute $1 million to a local air-quality cleanup project, after allegations of breaking water-quality and hazardous-waste laws on hundreds of occasions between the years of 1974 and 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p><p>In 2006, Honeywell announced that its decision to stop manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Mercury_switch" title="Mercury switch">mercury switches</a> had resulted in reductions of more than 11,300 kg, 2800 kg, and 1500 kg respectively of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, lead, and <a href="/wiki/Chromic_acid" title="Chromic acid">chromic acid</a> usage. The largest reduction represents 5% of mercury use in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The EPA acknowledged Honeywell's leadership in reducing mercury use through a 2006 National Partnership for Environmental Priorities (NPEP) Achievement Award for discontinuing the manufacturing of mercury switches.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvey_Cox_(1973).jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Harvey_Cox_%281973%29.jpg/220px-Harvey_Cox_%281973%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1304" data-file-height="1738"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 293px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Harvey_Cox_%281973%29.jpg/220px-Harvey_Cox_%281973%29.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="293" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvey_Cox_(1973).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harvey_Cox" title="Harvey Cox">Harvey Cox</a> holding a Honeywell <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(weaponry)" title="Fragmentation (weaponry)">fragmentation</a> bomb (1973)</div></div></div>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Criticism" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<p>On March 10, 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a> reported that Honeywell was one of sixty companies that shielded annual profits from U.S. taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
In December 2011, the non-partisan liberal organization <a href="/wiki/Public_Campaign#Reports" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Campaign">Public Campaign</a> criticized <i>Honeywell International</i> for spending $18.3 million on <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">not paying any taxes</a> during 2008–2010, instead getting $34 million in tax rebates, despite making a profit of $4.9 billion, laying off 968 workers since 2008, and increasing executive pay by 15% to $54.2 million in 2010 for its top 5 executives.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p><p>Honeywell has also been criticized in the past for its manufacture of deadly and maiming weapons, such as <a href="/wiki/Cluster_bombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluster bombs">cluster bombs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">

<ul><li><span><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/28px-Industry5.svg.png" decoding="async" width="28" height="28" class="noviewer" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 28px;height: 28px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/28px-Industry5.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="28" data-height="28" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/42px-Industry5.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Industry5.svg/56px-Industry5.svg.png 2x" data-class="noviewer"> </span></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Companies" title="Portal:Companies">Companies portal</a></span></li></ul></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(10)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honeywell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-10 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-10">
